<p dir="auto">{: .note }
These are the lecture notes from the Fall 2022 version of this class, completely unedited. It covers content that was covered in Week 2 discussion; we're leaving this up for you to get more notes!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Table of Contents</h2><a id="user-content-table-of-contents" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Table of Contents" href="#table-of-contents"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">{: .no_toc }</p>
<p dir="auto">{:toc}</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>dummy item</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Currying (continued)</h3><a id="user-content-currying-continued" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Currying (continued)" href="#currying-continued"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">A pseudocode that represent the logic of currying can be written as:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="Curry(Function f)
  e = The expression/body of function f
  For each parameter p from right to left:
    f_temp = Define a new lambda function with:
      1. p as its only parameter
      2. e as its (expression)
    e = f_temp
return f_temp"><pre class="notranslate"><code>Curry(Function f)
  e = The expression/body of function f
  For each parameter p from right to left:
    f_temp = Define a new lambda function with:
      1. p as its only parameter
      2. e as its (expression)
    e = f_temp
return f_temp
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Let's use the procedure above to curry the function <code>mult3 x y z = x*y*z</code></p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>At first, <code>e</code> is equal to <code>x*y*z</code></li>
<li>Then we enter the for loop
<ul dir="auto">
<li>In the first iteration, <code>z</code> is the parameter to be handled. <code>f_temp = \z -&gt; x*y*z</code></li>
<li>Next, we handle <code>y</code> and <code>f_temp = \y -&gt; (\z -&gt; x*y*z)</code></li>
<li>Finally, we handle <code>y</code> and we get <code>f_temp = \x -&gt; (\y -&gt; (\z -&gt; x*y*z))</code></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Thus, the curried version <code>mult3c = \x -&gt; (\y -&gt; (\z -&gt; x*y*z))</code>.</p>
<p dir="auto">Let's see how we call our curried function with <code>x=2</code>, <code>y=3</code> and <code>z=5</code>!</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; mult3c = \x -&gt; (\y -&gt; (\z -&gt; x*y*z))
ghci&gt; f1 = mult3c 2
ghci&gt; f2 = f1 3
ghci&gt; result = f2 5
30"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; mult3c = \x -&gt; (\y -&gt; (\z -&gt; x*y*z))</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; f1 = mult3c 2</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; f2 = f1 3</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; result = f2 5</span>
<span class="pl-c1">30</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">So, what actually are <code>f1</code> and <code>f2</code>?</p>
<p dir="auto"><code>f1</code> is obtained by calling the lambda of <code>mult3c</code> with <code>x=2</code>. Thus we replace the occurrences of <code>x</code> inside the lambda function with 2 and remove the <code>\x -&gt;</code> at the beginning. <code>f1</code> is: <code>\y -&gt; (\z -&gt; 2*y*z)</code>.</p>
<p dir="auto">{: .warning }
When a inner lambda has the same parameter name as the outer one. The inner one takes precedence and <em>shadows</em> the outer one. In this case, the shadowed parameter will not get replaced. For example, when we evaluate <code>(\x -&gt; ((\x -&gt; x) (x + 1))) 3</code>, we will get <code>((\x -&gt; x) (3 + 1))</code>.</p>
<p dir="auto">Once again, when we repeat the process, we can get <code>f2 = \z -&gt; (2*3*z)</code>, and finally <code>result = 2*3*5</code>, thus 30.</p>
<p dir="auto">In fact, every time you define a function of more than parameter in Haskell, Haskell automatically and invisibly <strong>curries it</strong> for you.</p>
<p dir="auto">This is evidenced by the fact that even if you replace the <code>mult3c</code> above with the "normal" <code>mult3 x y z = x*y*z</code>, you will get the same result. You can also legally execute <code>(((mult3 2) 3) 5)</code> in Haskell and get 30.</p>
<p dir="auto">Surprising fact: Our usual way of calling functions <code>mult3 2 3 5</code> looks like a <em>single call</em> with three arguments, but it's really <em>three calls</em> to three different functions! What happen's is exactly identical to <code>(((mult3 2) 3) 5)</code>, thus we know function invocation is <em>left associative</em>.</p>
<p dir="auto">Also, now we can reveal the fact about Haskell function type signatures:</p>
<p dir="auto">Remember those ugly (and maybe confusing) Haskell type signatures?</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="mult3 :: Double -&gt; Double -&gt; Double -&gt; Double
mult3 x y z = x * y * z"><pre><span class="pl-en">mult3</span> <span class="pl-k">::</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span>
mult3 x y z <span class="pl-k">=</span> x <span class="pl-k">*</span> y <span class="pl-k">*</span> z</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Well, here's what they should really look like given that all functions are curried (thus <code>mult3</code> is a three-level nested function that returns a function at each outer level):</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="mult3 :: Double -&gt; (Double -&gt; (Double -&gt; Double))
mult3 x y z = x * y * z"><pre><span class="pl-en">mult3</span> <span class="pl-k">::</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> (<span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> (<span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span>))
mult3 x y z <span class="pl-k">=</span> x <span class="pl-k">*</span> y <span class="pl-k">*</span> z</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Although the latter form looks more <em>fundamental</em>, it is even uglier. That's why we used smore syntactic sugar to remove the parentheses. (We can regard the <code>-&gt;</code> "operator" in type signatures as <strong>right-associative</strong>)</p>
<p dir="auto">People may ask, why does Haskell curry function? We think there are two primary reasons:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>First, it enables partial function application (which we we'll see more below)</li>
<li>But mostly it appears to be motivated by theorists! It can facilitate program analysis and other stuff and it is consistent with the <em>lambda calculus</em>, the mathematical foundation of functional programming.</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Partial Function Application</h3><a id="user-content-partial-function-application" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Partial Function Application" href="#partial-function-application"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">When you call a function with less than the full number of arguments this is called "Partial Function Application" or "Partial Application."</p>
<p dir="auto">Formally speaking, partial function application is an operation where we define a new function <code>g</code> by combining:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>An existing function <code>f</code> that takes one or more arguments, with</li>
<li>Default values for one or more of those arguments</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">The new function <code>g</code> is a specialization of <code>f</code>, with hard-coded values for some of <code>f</code>'s parameters.</p>
<p dir="auto">Once defined, we can then call <code>g</code> with those arguments that have not yet been hard-coded.</p>
<p dir="auto">Partial function application is deeply related with currying we have just learned. In fact, when we used <code>((mult3 2) 3) 5</code> earlier, we are implicitly doing partial function application. But let's see more examples:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; mult3 x y z = x*y*z
ghci&gt; partial = mult3 2
ghci&gt; partial 3 5
30
ghci&gt; multby10_20 = mult3 10 20
ghci&gt; multby10_20 3"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; mult3 x y z = x*y*z</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; partial = mult3 2</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; partial 3 5</span>
<span class="pl-c1">30</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; multby10_20 = mult3 10 20</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; multby10_20 3</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Here, we are creating two specialized functions <code>partial y z = 2*y*z</code> and <code>multby10_20 z = 10*20*z</code> with partial application.</p>
<p dir="auto">Partial function applications can be useful when combined with pre-existing functions and operators. Let's see the following examples:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; add5 = (+) 5
ghci&gt; add5 100
105"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; add5 = (+) 5</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; add5 100</span>
<span class="pl-c1">105</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">All of Haskell's operators are just functions! So the <code>+</code> sign is basically a function of two arguments.</p>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
Surrounding an infix operator with a pair of parentheses converts it to the prefix form which gives you identical behavior to a normal function.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; map (/ 10) [100,200,300]
[10.0,20.0,30.0]
ghci&gt; map (10 /) [100,200,300]
[0.1,5.0e-2,3.333333333333333e-2]"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; map (/ 10) [100,200,300]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">[10.0,20.0,30.0]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; map (10 /) [100,200,300]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">[0.1,5.0e-2,3.333333333333333e-2]</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Haskell also allows you to apply partial application to either operands of infix operators. The function produced by the first line is equivalent to <code>\x -&gt; x / 10</code>, while the second line produces <code>\x -&gt; 10 / x</code>. You can see the behavior by reading the result returned by the <code>map</code> function.</p>
<p dir="auto">Here are more examples:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; filter (&gt;= 6) [2,4,6,8,10,3]
[6,8,10]
ghci&gt; map (++ &quot; is LIT!&quot;) [&quot;CS32&quot;,&quot;CS131&quot;]
[&quot;CS32 is LIT!&quot;,&quot;CS131 is LIT!&quot;]
ghci&gt; filter (`elem` ['A'..'Z']) &quot;Not Every Resistor Drives current&quot;
&quot;NERD&quot;"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; filter (&gt;= 6) [2,4,6,8,10,3]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">[6,8,10]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; map (++ " is LIT!") ["CS32","CS131"]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">["CS32 is LIT!","CS131 is LIT!"]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; filter (`elem` ['A'..'Z']) "Not Every Resistor Drives current"</span>
<span class="pl-c1">"NERD"</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">In the last example, the pair of backticks turns a function with two arguments into an infix operator so that you can supply the second argument with partial application.</p>
<p dir="auto">We can even use partial function application to define a partially-specified mapper function:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; cuber = map (\x -&gt; x^3)
ghci&gt; cuber [2,3,5]
[8,27,125]"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; cuber = map (\x -&gt; x^3)</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; cuber [2,3,5]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">[8,27,125]</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">As you can see, with partial function applications often we don't have to define a full function or lambda to do complex processing!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Algebraic Data Types</h3><a id="user-content-algebraic-data-types" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Algebraic Data Types" href="#algebraic-data-types"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Algebraic Data Type (ADT) is the term we use in functional programming to describe a user-defined data type that can have multiple fields.</p>
<p dir="auto">The closest thing to algebraic data types would be C++ structs and enumerated types. We use algebraic data types to create complex data structures like trees.</p>
<p dir="auto">In Haskell, we use keyword "data" to define algebraic data types.</p>
<p dir="auto">The simplest algebraic data types are just like C++ enums:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>The pipe (|) character means "OR"</li>
<li>Each choice for a given type is called a "variant" or a "kind"</li>
</ul>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="data Drink = Water | Coke | Sprite | Redbull
data Veggie = Broccoli | Lettuce | Tomato
data Protein = Eggs | Beef | Chicken | Beans"><pre><span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Drink</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent">Water</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Coke</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Sprite</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Redbull</span>
<span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Veggie</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent">Broccoli</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Lettuce</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Tomato</span>
<span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Protein</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent">Eggs</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Beef</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Chicken</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Beans</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">For the <code>Protein</code> type, its equivalent in C++ would be:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="enum Protein { Eggs, beef, chicken, Beans };"><pre><span class="pl-k">enum</span> Protein { Eggs, beef, chicken, Beans };</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">We can also define more complex ADTs where each variant also has one or more fields (like C++ struct).</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="data Meal =
 Breakfast Drink Protein |
 Lunch Drink Protein Veggie |
 Dinner Drink Protein Protein Veggie |
 Fasting"><pre><span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Meal</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span>
 <span class="pl-ent">Breakfast</span> <span class="pl-en">Drink</span> <span class="pl-en">Protein</span> |
 <span class="pl-ent">Lunch</span> <span class="pl-en">Drink</span> <span class="pl-en">Protein</span> <span class="pl-en">Veggie</span> |
 <span class="pl-ent">Dinner</span> <span class="pl-en">Drink</span> <span class="pl-en">Protein</span> <span class="pl-en">Protein</span> <span class="pl-en">Veggie</span> |
 <span class="pl-ent">Fasting</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">The definition of first variant <code>Breakfast</code> means that it is comprised of <code>Drink</code> and <code>Protein</code> fields. Its equivalent in C++ would be:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="struct Breakfast {
  Drink d;
  Protein p;
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">struct</span> <span class="pl-en">Breakfast</span> {
  Drink d;
  Protein p;
}<span class="pl-ii"></span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">The only difference is, the fields in Haskell does not have a name, and they are distuished by the order they appear in the definition.</p>
<p dir="auto">Note that the variants with and without fields can coexist within a single ADT, the <code>Fasting</code> above is just an example.</p>
<p dir="auto">Here are some examples to define variables with ADT:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="careys_meal = Breakfast Redbull Eggs
pauls_meal = Lunch Water Chicken Broccoli
davids_meal = Fasting"><pre>careys_meal <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent">Breakfast</span> <span class="pl-ent">Redbull</span> <span class="pl-ent">Eggs</span>
pauls_meal <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent">Lunch</span> <span class="pl-ent">Water</span> <span class="pl-ent">Chicken</span> <span class="pl-ent">Broccoli</span>
davids_meal <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent">Fasting</span></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Syntax</h4><a id="user-content-syntax" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Syntax" href="#syntax"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">We use another example to introduce the syntax of Algebraic Data Types</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="-- algebraic.hs
data Color = Red | Green | Blue

data Shape =
  Circle
   Float Float -- x, y
   Float       -- radius
   Color |
  Rectangle
   Float Float -- x1, y1
   Float Float -- x2, y2
   Color"><pre><span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> algebraic.hs</span>
<span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Color</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent">Red</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Green</span> | <span class="pl-ent">Blue</span>

<span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Shape</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span>
  <span class="pl-ent">Circle</span>
   <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Float</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Float</span></span> <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> x, y</span>
   <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Float</span></span>       <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> radius</span>
   <span class="pl-en">Color</span> |
  <span class="pl-ent">Rectangle</span>
   <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Float</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Float</span></span> <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> x1, y1</span>
   <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Float</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Float</span></span> <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> x2, y2</span>
   <span class="pl-en">Color</span></pre></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>To define a new ADT, we use the <code>data</code> keyword</li>
<li>The syntax can be represented as <code>data TypeName = Variant1 | Variant2 | ... | VariantN</code></li>
<li>The initial of type names should be <em>capitalized</em> (to differentiate with type variables)</li>
<li>The initial of variant names should be capitalized as well.</li>
<li>The general syntax for defining a variant that has fields is: <code>VariantName Type1 Type2 ... TypeN</code></li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Each time you define a variant with fields, Haskell implicitly creats a "constructor" that can be used to create a new variable. For example, the code above a <code>Circle</code> constructor with four parameters (three Floats and a Color) to create a new <code>Circle</code>.</p>
<p dir="auto">We can add the keywords <code>deriving Show</code> at the end of a definition to enable us to print out variable values in GHCi. This is simply a hack that makes our life easier when debugging in GHCi.</p>
<p dir="auto">If we added <code>deriving Show</code> to both <code>Color</code> and <code>Shape</code>, we can test with the types in GHCi:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; :load algebraic
[1 of 1] Compiling Main             ( lambda.hs, interpreted )
ghci&gt; c = Circle 5 6 10 Red
ghci&gt; c
Circle 5.0 6.0 10.0 Red
ghci&gt; r = Rectangle 0 0 5 6 Blue
ghci&gt; r
Rectangle 0.0 0.0 5.0 6.0 Blue
ghci&gt; :t r
r :: Shape
ghci&gt; colors = [Red, Red, Blue]
ghci&gt; colors
[Red,Red,Blue]"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; :load algebraic</span>
<span class="pl-c1">[1 of 1] Compiling Main             ( lambda.hs, interpreted )</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; c = Circle 5 6 10 Red</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; c</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Circle 5.0 6.0 10.0 Red</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; r = Rectangle 0 0 5 6 Blue</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; r</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Rectangle 0.0 0.0 5.0 6.0 Blue</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; :t r</span>
<span class="pl-c1">r :: Shape</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; colors = [Red, Red, Blue]</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; colors</span>
<span class="pl-c1">[Red,Red,Blue]</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Note that the fields of a variant have no names! That means we must use their order/position to identify them.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Pattern Matching with ADT</h4><a id="user-content-pattern-matching-with-adt" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Pattern Matching with ADT" href="#pattern-matching-with-adt"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">In functional languages, we use pattern matching to process algebraic variables.</p>
<p dir="auto">Let's see an example with our shapes. We first update our Shape algebraic type a bit and add a new "Shapeless" type of shape.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="-- shapes.hs

data Shape =
   Shapeless
 | Circle
    -- x      y    rad
    Double Double Double
 | Rectangle
    -- x1    y1     x2     y2
    Double Double Double Double
 deriving Show

getArea :: Shape -&gt; Double
getArea Shapeless = 0
getArea (Circle _ _ r) = pi * r^2
getArea (Rectangle x1 y1 x2 y2) =
  (abs (x2-x1)) * (abs (y2-y1))"><pre><span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> shapes.hs</span>

<span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Shape</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span>
   <span class="pl-ent">Shapeless</span>
 | <span class="pl-ent">Circle</span>
    <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> x      y    rad</span>
    <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span>
 | <span class="pl-ent">Rectangle</span>
    <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> x1    y1     x2     y2</span>
    <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span>
 <span class="pl-k">deriving</span> <span class="pl-e">Show</span>

<span class="pl-en">getArea</span> <span class="pl-k">::</span> <span class="pl-en">Shape</span> <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Double</span></span>
getArea <span class="pl-ent">Shapeless</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">0</span>
getArea (<span class="pl-ent">Circle</span> _ _ r) <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">pi</span> <span class="pl-k">*</span> r<span class="pl-k">^</span><span class="pl-c1">2</span>
getArea (<span class="pl-ent">Rectangle</span> x1 y1 x2 y2) <span class="pl-k">=</span>
  (<span class="pl-c1">abs</span> (x2<span class="pl-k">-</span>x1)) <span class="pl-k">*</span> (<span class="pl-c1">abs</span> (y2<span class="pl-k">-</span>y1))</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">We focus on the <code>getArea</code> function:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>The first line is the type signature. As this functions operates on various types of <code>Shape</code>, this definition makes sense.</li>
<li>Then follows the actual pattern matching
<ul dir="auto">
<li>The first line means: "If the user passes in a Shapeless variable, then always return zero".</li>
<li>The second line handles the <code>Circle</code> case. It gets the third field (radius), placing it into variable <code>r</code> and computes the area.</li>
<li>The last line handles <code>Rectangle</code>. It extracts the fields from the data structure and computes the area according to the width and height.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
We can combine pattern matching on ADT with lists and tuples to create complex patterns. For example, the following function counts the occurrences of <code>Circle</code> within a list of <code>Shape</code> without using <code>map</code>, <code>filter</code> or <code>foldl</code>.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="count_circle :: [Shape] -&gt; Integer
count_circle [] = 0
count_circle ((Circle _ _ _):xs) = 1 + count_circle xs
count_circle (_:xs) = count_circle xs"><pre><span class="pl-en">count_circle</span> <span class="pl-k">::</span> [<span class="pl-en">Shape</span>] <span class="pl-k">-&gt;</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">Integer</span></span>
count_circle <span class="pl-c1">[]</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">0</span>
count_circle ((<span class="pl-ent">Circle</span> _ _ _)<span class="pl-k">:</span>xs) <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">1</span> <span class="pl-k">+</span> count_circle xs
count_circle (_<span class="pl-k">:</span>xs) <span class="pl-k">=</span> count_circle xs</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Exercise: Implement count_circle with <code>map</code>, <code>filter</code> and <code>foldl</code> respectively.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Creating Trees with ADT</h4><a id="user-content-creating-trees-with-adt" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Creating Trees with ADT" href="#creating-trees-with-adt"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Let's examine a more interesting use of ADTs – creating a binary search tree.</p>
<p dir="auto">We'll start by looking at the definition of a tree node ADT.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="-- bst.hs
data Tree =
  Nil |
  Node String Tree Tree"><pre><span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">--</span> bst.hs</span>
<span class="pl-k">data</span> <span class="pl-en">Tree</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span>
  <span class="pl-ent">Nil</span> |
  <span class="pl-ent">Node</span> <span class="pl-en"><span class="pl-c1">String</span></span> <span class="pl-en">Tree</span> <span class="pl-en">Tree</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">A tree data type has two possible variants:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>An empty variant indicating that there is no node, like a <code>nullptr</code></li>
<li>A node variant representing actual nodes.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">In this definition, each Node holds a String value, and two other tree data items that can be either <code>Nil</code> or <code>Node</code>. The first one is the left child of the node, and the second one is the right child. So this is a binary tree.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-text-shell-session notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="ghci&gt; :load bst
[1 of 1] Compiling Main             ( bst.hs, interpreted )
ghci&gt; empty_tree = Nil
ghci&gt; one_node = Node &quot;Lit&quot; Nil Nil
ghci&gt; r = Rectangle 0 0 5 6 Blue
ghci&gt; left_child = Node &quot;Boomer&quot; Nil Nil
ghci&gt; right_child = Node &quot;Zoomer&quot; Nil Nil
ghci&gt; root = Node &quot;Cheugy&quot; left_child right_child
ghci&gt; root
Node &quot;Cheugy&quot; (Node &quot;Boomer&quot; Nil Nil) (Node &quot;Zoomer&quot; Nil Nil)"><pre><span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; :load bst</span>
<span class="pl-c1">[1 of 1] Compiling Main             ( bst.hs, interpreted )</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; empty_tree = Nil</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; one_node = Node "Lit" Nil Nil</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; r = Rectangle 0 0 5 6 Blue</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; left_child = Node "Boomer" Nil Nil</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; right_child = Node "Zoomer" Nil Nil</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; root = Node "Cheugy" left_child right_child</span>
<span class="pl-c1">ghci&gt; root</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Node "Cheugy" (Node "Boomer" Nil Nil) (Node "Zoomer" Nil Nil)</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">In the end of the code above, we created a tree with three nodes.</p>
<p dir="auto">With this tree structure, we can implement search algorithm on this BST.</p>
<p dir="auto">We'll use a classic recursive search function and pattern matching.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="search Nil val = False
search (Node curval left right) val
 | val == curval = True
 | val &lt; curval = search left val
 | otherwise = search right val"><pre>search <span class="pl-ent">Nil</span> val <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent"><span class="pl-c1">False</span></span>
search (<span class="pl-ent">Node</span> curval left right) val
 <span class="pl-k">|</span> val <span class="pl-k">==</span> curval <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-ent"><span class="pl-c1">True</span></span>
 <span class="pl-k">|</span> val <span class="pl-k">&lt;</span> curval <span class="pl-k">=</span> search left val
 <span class="pl-k">|</span> <span class="pl-c1">otherwise</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> search right val</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Ok, now let's see how to create a function that adds a new node to a tree.</p>
<p dir="auto">But this isn't so easy, because in functional languages, we can't modify existing values/variables. Suppose we create a node N and we want to make it the child of existing node M. So we can't just find a node M and change its left or right child to point to the new N!</p>
<p dir="auto">The strategy is, we want to create a new node M', let it point to N and replace the node M we need to change.</p>
<p dir="auto">But wait... Now the new node M is not part of the tree either. But we can just carry it on to create a new node to replace M's parent. Eventually we can do this all the way to the root, and at that point, we can forget about the old tree.</p>
<p dir="auto">The following Haskell code implements the logic:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-haskell notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="insert Nil val =
  Node val Nil Nil

insert (Node curval left right) val
  | val == curval =
      Node curval left right
  | val &lt; curval =
      Node curval (insert left val) right
  | val &gt; curval =
      Node curval left (insert right val)"><pre>insert <span class="pl-ent">Nil</span> val <span class="pl-k">=</span>
  <span class="pl-ent">Node</span> val <span class="pl-ent">Nil</span> <span class="pl-ent">Nil</span>

insert (<span class="pl-ent">Node</span> curval left right) val
  <span class="pl-k">|</span> val <span class="pl-k">==</span> curval <span class="pl-k">=</span>
      <span class="pl-ent">Node</span> curval left right
  <span class="pl-k">|</span> val <span class="pl-k">&lt;</span> curval <span class="pl-k">=</span>
      <span class="pl-ent">Node</span> curval (insert left val) right
  <span class="pl-k">|</span> val <span class="pl-k">&gt;</span> curval <span class="pl-k">=</span>
      <span class="pl-ent">Node</span> curval left (insert right val)</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">This method sounds a little bit wasteful, because we need to create new nodes all the way up. However, if we think carefully about the process, we'll find that to add a new node, we just need to generate replacement nodes for the nodes between our new node and the root. Then if a tree is balanced with <code>n</code> nodes, we need to create just <code>log2(n)</code> new nodes!</p>
<p dir="auto">Meanwhile, with the creation of the new node, the old nodes will no longer be used. This is where "Garbage Collection" comes in.</p>
<p dir="auto">Garbage Collection is a language feature that automatically reclaims unused variables, and all functional languages have built-in Garbage Collection.</p>
<p dir="auto">We'll learn more about GC later in the course, but the important thing is that GC doesn't impact Big-O!</p>
<p dir="auto">Although here we see that immutable tree is efficient (in the sense it does not change the Big-O bound of operations). It does not apply to all data structures.</p>
<p dir="auto">For example, if hash tables are implemented as immutable, the insertion will be inefficient because you must regenerate the full array of n buckets every time you add a new item.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Influencer Alert: Immutability</h4><a id="user-content-influencer-alert-immutability" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Influencer Alert: Immutability" href="#influencer-alert-immutability"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Many languages now provide immutable data structures – it helps simplify code, reduce bugs, and ease multi-processing.</p>
<p dir="auto">Examples include Google's Guava library for Java, and immutable.js for JavaScript.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-java notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="// Java Guava immutability example
Set&lt;Integer&gt; oldSet = ImmutableSet.of(1,2,3,4);

// Generate a whole new set, with 5 added to it
Set&lt;Integer&gt; newSet = new ImmutableSet.Builder&lt;Integer&gt;().addAll(oldSet)
                               .add(5)
                               .build();"><pre><span class="pl-c">// Java Guava immutability example</span>
<span class="pl-smi">Set</span>&lt;<span class="pl-smi">Integer</span>&gt; <span class="pl-s1">oldSet</span> = <span class="pl-smi">ImmutableSet</span>.<span class="pl-en">of</span>(<span class="pl-c1">1</span>,<span class="pl-c1">2</span>,<span class="pl-c1">3</span>,<span class="pl-c1">4</span>);

<span class="pl-c">// Generate a whole new set, with 5 added to it</span>
<span class="pl-smi">Set</span>&lt;<span class="pl-smi">Integer</span>&gt; <span class="pl-s1">newSet</span> = <span class="pl-k">new</span> <span class="pl-smi">ImmutableSet</span>.<span class="pl-smi">Builder</span>&lt;<span class="pl-smi">Integer</span>&gt;().<span class="pl-en">addAll</span>(<span class="pl-s1">oldSet</span>)
                               .<span class="pl-en">add</span>(<span class="pl-c1">5</span>)
                               .<span class="pl-en">build</span>();</pre></div>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-js notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="// JavaScript immutable.js example

// Create a new immutable set of 1-4
const oldSet = Immutable.Set([1,2,3,4]);

// add returns a new set &amp; doesn't change orig.
const newSet = oldSet.add(5)"><pre><span class="pl-c">// JavaScript immutable.js example</span>

<span class="pl-c">// Create a new immutable set of 1-4</span>
<span class="pl-k">const</span> <span class="pl-s1">oldSet</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-v">Immutable</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-en">Set</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-kos">[</span><span class="pl-c1">1</span><span class="pl-kos">,</span><span class="pl-c1">2</span><span class="pl-kos">,</span><span class="pl-c1">3</span><span class="pl-kos">,</span><span class="pl-c1">4</span><span class="pl-kos">]</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span><span class="pl-kos">;</span>

<span class="pl-c">// add returns a new set &amp; doesn't change orig.</span>
<span class="pl-k">const</span> <span class="pl-s1">newSet</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s1">oldSet</span><span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-en">add</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-c1">5</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Summary</h3><a id="user-content-summary" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Summary" href="#summary"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">In summary, here are what we have learned about functional programming</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Every function must take an argument.</li>
<li>Every function must return a value.</li>
<li>Functions are "pure" and have no side effects</li>
<li>Calling the same function with the same input always returns the same result.</li>
<li>All variables are "immutable" and can never be modified!</li>
<li>Functions are just like any other data and can be stored in variables and passed as arguments.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">And as we have seen along the way, while there are no purely functional languages in mainstream use, the paradigm has influenced every major language.</p>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
This concludes our introduction to Haskell and functional programming. Hope you had fun!</p>
